        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.4c09706"><meta property="og:title" content="Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/acsami.4c09706.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="This research highlights the efficacy of NaNbO3 as a coating for P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 cathodes in sodium-ion batteries. The coating enhances the kinetic behavior and cyclability of the electrochemical cells, as shown by electrochemical measurements. XRD analysis indicates that Nb does not incorporate into the cathode structure, implying a physical interaction between the coating and the cathode material. XRF analysis and EDX mapping confirm the actual composition and uniform dispersion of elements throughout the sample, while the electron micrographs evidence the occurrence of NaNbO3 particles modifying the surface of the layered oxide. The Ni4+/Ni3+ and Ni3+/Ni2+ redox pairs, along with the partially reversible oxidation of oxide to peroxide anions, contribute significantly to cell capacity, as revealed by XPS spectra. This last effect and the appearance of a co-intercalated phase at high voltage are positive factors to provide fast kinetics. Cyclic voltammograms show that samples coated with 2–3% NaNbO3 have superior rate capability, with high capacitive response and apparent diffusion coefficients. These samples also have low impedance at the electrode–electrolyte interface, which helps deliver a high capacity at 5C. Further cycling at 1C shows improved cyclability in the bare and 3% coated samples, due to their higher diffusion coefficients on charging. Notably, the 3% NaNbO3-coated sample exhibits excellent cyclability below 0 °C, making it a promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/acsami.4c09706.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931884f9dabf54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931884f9dabf54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '831a09bd-7425-42bc-b29c-9c6db28cbd39'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="https://www2.snapengage.com/chatjs/ServiceGetConfig?w=921a4ec7-cecf-43a6-afa5-6cd1346520b1"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/article-dc83549a302b9ecf025f.js"></script><script charset="utf-8" async="" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931884f9dabf54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="931884f9dabf54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improving the Performance of the Layered Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathode of Sodium-Ion Batteries by Direct Coating with Sodium Niobium Oxide", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c09706", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0012.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">This research highlights the efficacy of NaNbO<sub>3</sub> as a coating for P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes in sodium-ion batteries. The coating enhances the kinetic behavior and cyclability of the electrochemical cells, as shown by electrochemical measurements. XRD analysis indicates that Nb does not incorporate into the cathode structure, implying a physical interaction between the coating and the cathode material. XRF analysis and EDX mapping confirm the actual composition and uniform dispersion of elements throughout the sample, while the electron micrographs evidence the occurrence of NaNbO<sub>3</sub> particles modifying the surface of the layered oxide. The Ni<sup>4+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>2+</sup> redox pairs, along with the partially reversible oxidation of oxide to peroxide anions, contribute significantly to cell capacity, as revealed by XPS spectra. This last effect and the appearance of a co-intercalated phase at high voltage are positive factors to provide fast kinetics. Cyclic voltammograms show that samples coated with 2–3% NaNbO<sub>3</sub> have superior rate capability, with high capacitive response and apparent diffusion coefficients. These samples also have low impedance at the electrode–electrolyte interface, which helps deliver a high capacity at 5C. Further cycling at 1C shows improved cyclability in the bare and 3% coated samples, due to their higher diffusion coefficients on charging. Notably, the 3% NaNbO<sub>3</sub>-coated sample exhibits excellent cyclability below 0 °C, making it a promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under</p><div class="article__cc-license__inter"><div class="article__cc-license--hoverable"><a href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" aria-describedby="tooltip-cc-text" class="link--bold" data-id="article_abstract_CC-License">CC-BY 4.0
                                    <span class="arrow"></span></a>
                                .

                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Coating&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_coating">coating</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layered+Oxides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layered%20oxides">layered oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nickel&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nickel">nickel</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" oxide</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Early concerns regarding the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries (SIB) as competitors to lithium-ion batteries (LIB) gradually disappear. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> The inherent lesser negative reduction potential and larger size of sodium than lithium initially pointed out as a critical limitation to achieve enough performing batteries. Notwithstanding, the research on new electrode materials has achieved relevant breakthroughs that have eventually attracted the market attention to sodium-ion batteries. Especially, Prussian blue analogues, metal sulfides, metal–organic frameworks, NASICON-type phosphates, and layered metal oxides have demonstrated that their open frameworks are suitable to ensure fast ion diffusion at high working voltages to play the role of cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 6 to 11">(6−11)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Energy density is no longer the primary indicator to pursue. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) can be more cost-effective than lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the abundance and uniform distribution of sodium in the Earth’s crust. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 12 to 14">(12−14)</a> Moreover, efficient procedures for extracting sodium from seawater are known. Additionally, SIBs exhibit lower tendencies to form dendrites, which enhances their stability and safety during cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Specifically, different reports envision SIB application in large-scale grid storage to increase the integration of renewable energy sources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 15 to 19">(15−19)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Layered oxides with a Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> (where 0.67 ⩽ x ⩽ 1) nominal stoichiometry, where M represents transition elements such as Mn, Fe, Ni, etc., exhibit significantly high output voltage, large theoretical capacity, and simple synthetic routes. These characteristics make them attractive cathode materials for practical SIBs. Their typical structures belong to the O3-type and P2-type crystal families. Both types have advantages and limitations, making it challenging to determine the most suitable structure for eventual commercial use. Notably, sodium diffusivity is better in the P2-type phase, thanks to low-energy conduction pathways where Na<sup>+</sup> ions, located at triangular prism sites within the interlayer space, migrate directly through rectangular faces. In contrast, Na<sup>+</sup> ions transport through small triangular faces in an O3-type structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is a promising positive electrode. Transition metals and sodium are in alternating slabs, allowing Na<sup>+</sup> ions to migrate through low-energy conduction pathways created by triangular prism sites sharing faces. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Its high output voltage, large capacity, moisture resilience, and ease of large-scale manufacturing make this oxide an appealing cathode material for practical sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> Despite its interesting features, challenging issues need to be overcome. The multiple voltage steps observed in the charge/discharge come from the phase transitions, resulting in various Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordered patterns that form at specific Na stoichiometries. This phenomenon is commonly attributed to the strong repulsion among Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the Na layer and charge ordering in the transition metal layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> It decreases the Na-ion diffusion coefficient and limits the dimensionality of Na-ion transport, harming the electrochemical properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Also, the capacity delivered from the 4.2 V plateau decays abruptly upon cycling, accompanied by a sharp increase in polarization. The occurrence of a biphasic region involving P2–O2 transitions leads to severe structural degradation and a dramatic loss of capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Several strategies are proposed to optimize cell performance. Our research group has experience with metal doping, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> partial replacement of nickel, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> materials with controlled morphology, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> and new preparative routes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> Alternatively, surface modification is a feasible approach to enhance the electrochemical performance of cathodes by improving cyclability at high rates, avoiding electrode degradation, and mitigating undesirable phase transitions. For instance, CeO<sub>2</sub> coating stabilizes the redox reaction of lattice oxygen above 4.2 V, contributing favorably to a large and stable reversible capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Additionally, Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating on Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> suppresses unfavorable high voltage side reactions and prevents metal oxide layer exfoliation, resulting in enhanced cycling performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">These results prompted us to seek an effective novel surface modifier to improve the electrochemical behavior of this layered oxide. In this way, sodium niobate (NaNbO<sub>3</sub>), with a perovskite-type structure, features interesting properties in applications such as piezoelectricity, ferroelectricity, and photocatalysis. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> Although its open structure and rich redox chemistry (Nb<sup>5+</sup>/Nb<sup>4+</sup>/Nb<sup>3+</sup>) would favor high specific capacity as an anode, its poor electrical conductivity has limited its applicability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> Recently, Zhou et al. reported that attempts to dope a layered oxide with Nb resulted in an unexpected surface reorganization of an atomic-scale NaNbO<sub>3</sub> coating layer that effectively prevented the dissolution of metals and surface side reactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">This study aims to evaluate, for the first time, the effect of NaNbO<sub>3</sub> coating on P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Several samples with varying contents of the coating agent were prepared, and their effects on textural and structural properties will be examined using X-ray diffraction, electron microscopy, and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy. Sodium half-cells, assembled with the studied material as working electrodes using both cyclic voltammetry and galvanostatic techniques are used to unveil the reliability of the optimized material as a cathode for Na-ion cells.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Materials and Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p">A sample with P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMO@Nb0) nominal composition was obtained by a sol–gel procedure. The sol–gel synthesis resided on the use of citric acid (Synth, 99%) as a chelating agent in a 3 to 2, citric to metal ratio. Thus, stoichiometric amounts of Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.6H<sub>2</sub>O (Sigma-Aldrich, 99%), Mn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.4H<sub>2</sub>O (Alfa Aesar, 98%), were dissolved in 100 mL containing the citric acid solution (solution A). Finally, sodium acetate with 5% excess (Vetec, 99%), was dissolved in 50 mL of distilled water (Solution B). Solution B was slowly dropwise into solution A, until complete mixing of the solutions. The solution was evaporated at 120 °C and the resulting gel calcinated at 450 °C for 3h. The obtained materials were uniaxially pressed into pellets and annealed in an alumina crucible at 900 °C with a 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup> heating rate for 12h.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The NaNbO<sub>3</sub> resin was synthesized by the Pechini method. The polymeric resin was produced using 17.7 g of citric acid (Synth 98%), used as a chelating agent, and 11.8 g of ethylene glycol (Synth) as a polymerizing reagent. The precursors of the NaNbO<sub>3</sub> phase were 5.97g niobium ammonium oxalate (NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>NbO(C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.3H<sub>2</sub>O), provided by CBMM (Brazilian Metallurgy and Mining Company), and 1.29g of sodium nitrate (Synth 98%). Finally, the viscosity of the resin was adjusted to 22 cP using a Brookfield viscometer and subjected to a gravimetric test to determine the amount of NaNbO<sub>3</sub>/ml of resin. In a second step, 500 mg of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub> were coated by a chemical route involving the addition of 1%, 2%, and 3% of NaNbO<sub>3</sub> resin in 50 mL of ethanol (Synth 99% v/v) using a pulse-ultrasonic probe (Ultronique) immersed in the precursor solution for 30 min. The stirring conditions were set at 750 W 20 kHz and a pulse duration of 10 s. Afterward, the resulting material was further dried at 120 °C overnight, pestled in an agate mortar, and annealed at 800 °C with a 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> heating rate for 2 h. These samples will be labeled as NMO@Nb(%) as follows: NMO@Nb1, NMO@Nb2, and NMO@Nb3.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Structural and Morphological Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray diffraction patterns were scanned between 10 and 80° (2-theta) at 1°/min by using an XRD-6000 Shimadzu diffractometer furnished with Cu Ka radiation and a graphite monochromator. TOPAS software was useful to determine the unit cell parameters. Field Emission-Scanning Electron micrographs (FE-SEM) and EDX maps were recorded in a JSM-7800F Prime JEOL Microscope. High-Resolution Transmission Electron Microscopy (HRTEM) images were recorded in a TALOS F200i. The elemental composition was determined using a Rigaku ZSX Primus IV sequential wavelength dispersive X-ray fluorescence (XRF) spectrometer.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Swagelok-type sodium half-cells were composed of working electrodes (approximately 3 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>) consisting of the following components: Active material (70%), Acetylene black (20%), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) (10%), mixed in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (Emplura, 99.5%) and gently stirred for at least 2 h. The resulting homogeneous paste was spread onto aluminum disks (diameter = 9 mm) and then vacuum-dried at 120 °C for several hours. The counter electrode was metallic sodium (Panreac, 99.8%). The electrolyte solution consisted of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> (Strem, &gt; 99%) in propylene carbonate (PC) (Sigma-Aldrich, 99.7%) with 2% wt. FEC (fluoroethylene carbonate) (Sigma-Aldrich, 99%). This solution impregnated glass fiber disks (GF/A-Whatman) separators. The cells were assembled in an argon-filled MBraun glovebox, where O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O traces could be monitored. Galvanostat/Potentiostat multichannel systems (Biologic) monitored the electrochemical tests. The rate capability was determined by galvanostatic cycling from C/10 to 5C between 2.0 and 4.3 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. Cyclic voltammograms (CV) were scanned within the same potential window at rates ranging from 0.05 to 0.6 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. To unveil the cell’s internal impedance, Nyquist plots were recorded using Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) on an SP-150 Biologic. The open circuit voltage was perturbed with an oscillating signal (amplitude = 5 mV) from 100 kHz to 2 mHz. Cells that underwent one cycle at C/10 were allowed to relax for at least 12 h to reach a quasi-equilibrium state.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Figure S1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) shows the XRD pattern of the orthorhombic NaNbO<sub>3</sub> coating agent. The diagram was indexed in the <i>Pbcm</i> space group of the orthorhombic system, revealing its purity and crystallinity, along with cell parameters of a = 5.517(2) Å, b = 5.568(2) Å, and c = 15.533(4) Å. The diagrams of bare and coated samples feature a set of narrow reflections, indexed in the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> space group of the hexagonal system, corresponding to the structure of the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material used as an electrochemically active substrate (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). This material exhibits an open framework, consisting of alternate slabs of (Mn, Ni)O<sub>6</sub> octahedra and NaO<sub>6</sub> prisms sharing faces, which facilitates the diffusion of sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> Their lattice parameters, collected in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>, are like those reported in the literature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> As the coating level increases, these values remain unvaried, suggesting that the incorporation of Nb into the structure of the substrate cannot be inferred. As mentioned above, Zhou et al. reported that Nb doping in Na<sub>0.67</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.38</sub>Nb<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> led to the in situ formation of a protective NaNbO<sub>3</sub> film, due to the more negative formation energies of Nb<sup>5+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> in the surface as compared with the bulk. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Our results indicate that the opposite effect involving the incorporation of Nb into the framework departing from a depositing procedure is not favored. The presence of the NaNbO<sub>3</sub> phase is hardly visible due to the low contents applied. Anyway, the most intense reflection could be observed for NMO@Nb4 and was remarked by arrows in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table 2</a> shows the elemental composition of the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>/<sub>3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> substrate and NaNbO<sub>3</sub> content deposited onto the coated samples, determined by X-ray fluorescence. These values show a close similarity to the theoretical ones, evidencing the reliability of the synthetic procedure to obtain the materials with the expected stoichiometry.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. X-ray diffraction patterns of bare and NaNbO<sub>3</sub>-coated P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> samples (black dots). Calculated patterns (red) and differential curves (gray) are included for each sample. DIF patterns for P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (blue) and NaNbO<sub>3</sub> (green) are depicted. Arrows indicate the most intense reflection appearing for NaNbO<sub>3</sub>. Miller indices of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> are shown for NMO@Nb0.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.882(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">11.136(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">80.1(1)</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. Na, Mn, and Ni Contents from the Stoichiometric Ratio and Data Determined by XRF</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="char" char=".">1.11</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NMO@Nb2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.99</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NMO@Nb3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.91</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NMO@Nb4</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.88</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">FE-SEM images allowed us to unveil the morphology of the NaNbO<sub>3</sub> deposition (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). On increasing the NaNbO<sub>3</sub> content, the Nb signal becomes more evident through the entire particle surface. Otherwise, the appearance of larger Nb-containing particles is observed for NMO@Nb3 and NMO@Nb4. Lin et al. found that the effect of Nb doping on the layered structure of a related oxide is limited, predicting that it will be detrimental whether the concentration continues to increase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Figure S2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) shows the EDX spectra and elemental maps of every element for the studied coated samples. Unfortunately, this technique did not provide clear evidence of the formation of a NaNbO<sub>3</sub> coating layer. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images reveal distinct textures at the particle edges in a selected coated sample. Elemental analysis conducted in these regions indicates a concentrated accumulation of niobium (Nb) in a narrow zone near the surface. Meanwhile, other representative substrate elements extend throughout the entire particle (as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. a) FE-SEM micrographs and Nb EDX maps of NaNbO<sub>3</sub>-coated samples; b) HRTEM image with the inset showing the FFT of the bulk image corresponding to the ⟨001⟩ zone axis of NMO, and c) EDX maps of a surface region for NMO@Nb3.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Sodium half-cells assembled with the studied samples were subjected to galvanostatic cycling. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> shows the second and fifth cycles at C/10. The overall profiles feature charge and discharge plateaus between 2.8 and 3.9 V, which are commonly attributed to the Ni<sup>4+</sup>/Ni<sup>2+</sup> redox couple and Na-vacancy rearrangement. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> This region is responsible for the largest contribution to the cell capacity. Additionally, minor bands below 3 V resulted from a limited contribution of the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> couple to the overall capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Their limited contribution indicates an effective suppression of the deleterious Jahn–Teller effect, which occurs when a large content of trivalent manganese is present during reduction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Furthermore, reversible plateaus within the range of 3.9 to 4.3 V are observable. They may correspond to the reversible oxidation of oxide anions to peroxo species. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 to 48">(46−48)</a> To our knowledge, the use of NaNbO<sub>3</sub> as cathode material for sodium-ion batteries is yet unreported. In contrast, we could find in the literature a few reports about its applicability as anode in lithium-ion batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref49" aria-label="reference 35 and 49">(35,49)</a> In these reports, the faradic contributions corresponding to the reduction of Nb<sup>5+</sup> occur beneath 1.5 V, out of the voltage window chosen for this study. To verify the lack of electroactivity of the coating agent in our measurement conditions, a sodium half-cell, assembled with pure NaNbO<sub>3</sub>, was cycled between 1.5 and 4.5 V at a rate of C/10 (Figure S3; <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Notably, the recorded discharge capacity values below 7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> were a negligible percentage contribution to overall capacity. Otherwise, coated samples provided lower initial capacities, most probably due to the electrochemical inactivity of the coated agent. Despite this fact, NaNbO<sub>3</sub> exerted a beneficial effect by providing a better capacity for the next cycles in coated samples.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Second and fifth galvanostatic cycles for bare and NaNbO<sub>3</sub>-coated P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> samples recorded at C/10 between 2.0 and 4.3 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The better reversibility of the coated samples was also exhibited at higher rates. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> plots their rate capabilities from C/10 to 5C. The initial discharge capacity value for the bare sample was 144 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. It was 83% of the theoretical capacity in the assumption that all the 0.67 Na atoms per formula could be efficiently inserted. The effect of the deposition of NaNbO<sub>3</sub> on the first five cycles at C/10 was a decrease in the initial capacity (122 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for NMO@Nb3). However, the capacity retention after the first cycle progressively improved by increasing the level of deposition. Thus, the capacity of the bare sample diminished to 120.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, while that of NMO@Nb3 remained at 120.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. It had a notorious effect on the rate capability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>). On increasing the C rate, it appears that NMO@Nb2 and NMO@Nb3 can sustain the highest capacity values at 5C 46.4 and 45.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, while values lower than 40 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> were observed for NMO@Nb0 and NMO@Nb1. These values are in correspondence to those reported for related oxides (Table S2; <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> also collects the capacity values for further cycling at 1C. It revealed that the surface modification with 3% NaNbO<sub>3</sub> presents optimal capacity retention and the highest discharge capacity, overcoming the performance of the bare sample. As can be seen, the optimization of the percentage of coating agent is crucial.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Rate capability of sodium half-cells cycled between 2.0 and 4.3 V at several rates from C/10 to 5C and then 1C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To unveil the relevant kinetic factors contributing to the enhanced performance of coated samples, cyclic voltammograms at increasing sweep rates were recorded allowing us to determine the possible effects of these distinctive features on the kinetic response (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a). For this purpose, three anodic peaks (labeled as Peaks 1–3) and their cathodic counterparts (Peaks 4–6). The analysis of their current maxima (<i>I</i><sub><i>p</i></sub>) versus the sweep rate ν follows a linear relationship, as described by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="Equation 1">Equation 1</a>: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-5">log</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-6" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-7"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-8" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-9">I</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-10" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-11">p</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-12"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-13" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-18">v</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-19" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-20">log</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-21" style="margin-left: 0em; the peaks used for further calculations are labeled. Plots of b) <i>b</i>-values and c) apparent diffusion coefficients of sodium.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The <i>b</i>-values, inferred from the slope, take values from 0.5 to 1 (Figure S5, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Those values close to 0.5 indicate a predominant faradic diffusion-limited reaction, while those close to 1.0 are typical of a capacitive response. This capacitive behavior arises from the electrostatic interactions between the surface of the substrate and the Na<sup>+</sup> incoming and outgoing Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The deposition of a coating agent certainly modifies the textural properties of the metal oxide surface, thereby altering the activation barriers associated with the capacitive process and fast exchange of Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> The <i>b</i>-values displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b reveal a non-negligible capacitive behavior at those peaks attributable to the nickel reaction, which is responsible for the largest contribution to the overall charge and discharge capacity. However, the four-volt plateau exhibits a predominant faradic behavior. Regarding the effect of NaNbO<sub>3</sub> content, larger values were determined for samples with the highest amount of the coating agent.</div><div class="NLM_p">Cyclic voltammograms can also be an interesting source of data to calculate the apparent diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub class="stack"><i>Na</i><sup>+</sup></sub><sup class="stack"><i>app</i></sup>). Randles-Sevcik established a linear relationship between the current intensity at the peak maxima (<i>I</i><sub><i>p</i></sub>) and the square root of the sweep rate, according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="Equation 2">Equation 2</a>: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and
