id="MJXp-Span-62">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-67" style="vertical-align: 0.82em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-68">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-69">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-70">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-71">C</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-72"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-73" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-74">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-75" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-76">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-77">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-78">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>I</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>p</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>2.69</mn><mo>×</mo><msup><mrow><mn>10</mn></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>5</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mi>A</mi><msup><mrow><msubsup><mrow><mi>D</mi></mrow><mrow><msup><mrow><mi>N</mi><mi>a</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup></mrow><mrow><mi>a</mi><mi>p</mi><mi>p</mi></mrow></msubsup></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mi>C</mi><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">This equation is expressed in terms of the number of electrons transferred in the redox reaction (<i>n</i>), the electrode area (<i>A</i>), and the bulk concentration of electroactive Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>C</i>). From the slopes of the linear plots shown in Figure S6 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), the sodium diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub class="stack"><i>Na</i><sup>+</sup></sub><sup class="stack"><i>app</i></sup>) were determined and depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c. These coefficients took maxima values for peaks 2 and 5, while lesser values occur close to the upper and lower limit voltages. Commonly, the absence of vacancies or Na<sup>+</sup> at the beginning and the end of the insertion process, respectively, is the origin of the differences between peaks. The higher values were recorded for NMO@Nb2 and NMO@Nb3, while the lowest values were determined for the bare and NMO@Nb1 samples. To summarize, the high capacitive contribution and apparent diffusion coefficients promote a fast Na<sup>+</sup> exchange during cell cycling for NMO@Nb2 and NMO@Nb3 even at the highest studied rate.</div><div class="NLM_p">Focusing on the best-performing sample. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a> shows ex-situ XRD patterns recorded for the NMO@Nb3 sample partially charged and subsequently discharged. Charging at 3.65 V the P2-type structure coexists with a new phase, characterized by new peaks at ca. 12.5° and 25° 2θ. On charging over 4 V, these new peaks replace the reflections ascribable to the P2-type structure. These peaks are characteristic of a cointercalated phase in which solvent molecules appear within the sodium slabs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> characterized by a spacing close to 14 Å (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). Further discharge involved the retrieval of the P2 phase. Previous reports have revealed that the combined contribution of both the reversible electrolyte cointercalation and peroxo oxidation favor a fast-kinetics electrode behavior. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Ex-situ XRD patterns of NMO@Nb3 electrodes partially charged and subsequently discharged. Reflections coming from the Al support are marked as Al. In black, the <i>hkl</i> Miller indexes corresponding to the P2-type structure. In blue, new reflections ascribable to solvent co-intercalation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The chemical state of elements at the particle surface was determined by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> shows the survey spectrum evidencing the presence of the expected elements, besides the contribution of Na<sup>+</sup>. The absence of a signal splitting in the Na 1s subspectrum indicates that a significant difference in iconicity is undetectable between Na<sup>+</sup> belonging to either the layered oxide or the NaNbO<sub>3</sub>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a> shows the Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, Mn 2p, Nb 3d, and O 1s core level spectra. The Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub> profile for the raw material is composed of a band at 854.2 eV with a small shoulder at 856.1 eV respectively attributed to the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>. The presence of the latter valency should be associated with oxidative processes affecting particle surface. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> The Mn 2p profile features asymmetric Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> bands respectively located at 641.9 and 653.5 eV and their corresponding satellites, being ascribable to the presence of tetravalent manganese. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Finally, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a> shows the O 1s spectrum in which the presence of a large peak at 529.1 eV shreds of evidence lattice oxygen, while those oxygen atoms associated with surface adsorbed species appear at 531.8 and 535.5 eV. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> Otherwise, the Nb 3d subspectrum features two main bands at 206.5 eV (Nb 3d<sub>5/2</sub>) and 209.3 eV (Nb 3d<sub>3/2</sub>) attributed to the presence of pentavalent vanadium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. XPS spectra at the Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, Mn 2p, Nb 3d, and O 1s core levels recorded for raw NMO@Nb3 and selected charged and discharged electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The XPS spectra of charged and discharged electrodes recorded at the Ni 2p3/2 core level evidenced the participation of the Ni<sup>4+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>2+</sup> oxidation states along the electrochemical oxidation and further reversibility on discharge (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>). The observation of the Mn 2p subspectra did not show appreciable changes during charge, as expected from the unavailability of higher oxidation states to Mn. Further discharge unveiled a weak shoulder to low energy bindings belonging to minor amounts of Mn<sup>3+</sup>, as mentioned above. The changes at the O 1s core level spectra are mostly attributable to an increase of the surface adsorbed species, coming from the formation of a surface film. Otherwise, a small band at 530.3 eV can be deconvoluted in the spectrum recorded for the fully charged electrode, which commonly relates to the formation of peroxo species. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> This band further disappeared in the fully discharged electrode, evidencing the reversibility of the oxo/peroxo redox couple. Finally, the Nb 3d subspectra revealed the appearance of a new band of Nb 3d shifted to a higher energy binding after full charge, which eventually became predominant after full discharge. The possibility of assigning this phenomenon to a niobium oxidation is not feasible, as will be mentioned below. Otherwise, a change in ionicity may explain this observation. The detrimental presence of HF traces in the electrolyte can be efficiently scavenged according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="Equation 3">Equation 3</a>:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-79"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-80"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-81"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-82"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-83"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-84" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-85">NaNbO</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-86" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-87">3</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-88" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-89">6</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-90">HF</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-91"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-92" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">→</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-93"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-94">NaF</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-95" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-96"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-97" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-98">NbF</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-99" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-100">5</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-101" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-102">3</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-103"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-104" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-105">H</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-106" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-107">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-108">O</span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>NaNbO</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mn>6</mn><mi>HF</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>NaF</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>NbF</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>5</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mn>3</mn><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div>This transformation in metal fluorides significantly diminishes the acidity of the electrolyte, thereby postponing the onset of cathode corrosion during cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60 ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 60 to 62">(60−62)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The contribution of the four-volt plateau to the capacity retention was untangled by recording several cycles at different potential windows for the NMO@Nb0 and NMO@Nb0 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>). While noticeable differences were not observed when the upper cutoff voltage was set at 4.0 V, the increase of the upper limit to 4.5 V evidenced the good reversibility of this plateau for NMO@Nb3 as compared to the bare sample. Thus, a high Coulombic efficiency of this high voltage region can be also considered a beneficial factor to the cyclability of the coated sample.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0008.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Galvanostatic charge and discharge curves of sodium half-cells assembled with NMO@Nb0 and NMO@Nb3 recorded at 2–4.0 V, 2–4.5 V, and 1.5–4.5 V at C/10.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Because the NaNbO<sub>3</sub> coating involves significant changes at the particle surface, impedance spectra were recorded to unveil the influence of the resistance at the electrode–electrolyte interphase. Nyquist plots recorded after the first cycle at C/10 are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a. The semicircles at high and medium frequencies are currently related to capacitive and resistive phenomena occurring at the surface layer and charge-transfer reaction at the interphase. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/suppl_file/am4c09706_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> shows the equivalent circuit used to fit these plots and calculate the cell resistance at the surface layer (R<sub>sl</sub>) and charge-transfer reaction (R<sub>ct</sub>) (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>). Comparatively negligible values were recorded for the ohmic drop at the electrolyte (R<sub>el</sub>) and they will not be here discussed. Low impedance values were determined for NMO@Nb2 and NMO@Nb3, which may explain their better performance at the highest kinetic rate. Nyquist plots were also recorded after the 100th cycle at 1C. Figure The overall impedance decreased as a result of the progressive stabilization of the interphase during cycling to sodium migration. The most relevant result is the low R<sub>sl</sub> value for NMO@Nb3 which evidence the capability of the Nb-containing layer to exert its protective effect while preserving the sodium migration through the interphase.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0009.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. Nyquist plots of the studied samples recorded after a) the 1st cycle at C/10 and b) the 100th cycle at 1C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl3"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 3. align="center">NMO@Nb3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.010</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.070</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.500</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="center">NMO@Nb4</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.013</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.764</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.814</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Future batteries pose a challenge for widespread use in cold climates due to difficulties ensuring reliable operation below 0 °C. Slower electrochemical reactions at these temperatures lead to capacity loss and, last, cell failure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> Encouraged by the promising electrochemical performance of NMO@Nb3, we investigated its low-temperature cycling performance at −15 °C. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a> shows the capacity retention of the latter sample when cycled at 1C. The sodium half-cell delivered an initial discharge capacity of 70 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> that decreased to 61 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> exhibiting a good Coulombic efficiency.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0010.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. Extended galvanostatic cycling at −15 °C of a sodium half-cell assembled with NMO@Nb3 under a 1C rate.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">This study demonstrates the effectiveness of NaNbO<sub>3</sub> as a coating agent for P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes in sodium-ion batteries. The coating significantly improves both kinetic behavior and cyclability, as evidenced by electrochemical measurements. XRD analysis reveals no Nb incorporation into the cathode structure, suggesting a physical interaction between the coating and the cathode material. XRF analysis confirmed the expected chemical composition of the coated samples, while EDX mapping evidence the uniform dispersion of elements in the sample. Electron micrographs revealed the attachment of the coating particles to the surface of the layered particles of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. XPS spectra, taken at various points during the first cycle, indicated that the Ni<sup>4+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>2+</sup> redox pairs, along with the partially reversible oxidation of oxide to peroxide anions, were the primary contributors to cell capacity. Ex-situ XRD patterns evidenced the appearance of a reversible co-intercalated phase. Both the formation of the latter phase and peroxo anions are favorable factors inducing a fast kinetic. The analysis of cyclic voltammograms recorded at escalating sweep rates, both at the start and end of cycling revealed the kinetic attributes of the electrode materials under study. Samples coated with 2–3% NaNbO<sub>3</sub> demonstrated superior rate capability, as evidenced by their high capacitive contribution and apparent diffusion coefficients recorded after the first cycle at C/10. Their low impedance at the electrode–electrolyte interface undoubtedly aids in delivering the highest capacity at 5C. Further cycling at 1C revealed improved cyclability in the bare and 3% coated samples, which exhibited a superior behavior, potentially due to their higher diffusion coefficients on charging. Lastly, the excellent cyclability below 0 °C of the sample coated with 3% NaNbO<sub>3</sub> suggests that this material could be a promising cathode for sodium-ion batteries.</div><div class="NLM_p">As reported in the suggested reference for Li<sub>x</sub>NbO<sub>y</sub>, due to the strong Nb–O bond, the Nb-rich surface can form an electrochemically active protective layer against electrolyte corrosion and deleterious phase transition. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> This together with the fluoride scavenging process cause the enhancement in the electrode material’s performance (new <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>). However, due to the poor electronic conductivity of the coating <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> and its nonelectroactivity, large amounts are detrimental to the electrochemical performance (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>). The composition that represents a compromise of these antagonist effects is 3% NaNbO<sub>3</sub>.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/medium/am4c09706_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0011.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Representation of Sodium-Ion and Electron Transport through the NMO-NaNbO<sub>3</sub>-Electrolyte Interphases and Fluoride Scavenging Process</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09706/asset/images/large/am4c09706_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09706&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The research and industrialization progress and prospects of sodium ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Tianwei; Li, Guohua; Duan, Yi; Wu, Yanlong; Zhang, Tianhang; Zhao, Xuyang; Luo, Min; Liu, Yafei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the widespread use of elec. vehicles and large-scale energy storage applications, lithium-ion batteries will face the problem of resource shortage.  As a new type of secondary chem. power source, sodium ion battery has the advantages of abundant resources, low cost, high energy conversion efficiency, long cycle life, high safety, excellent high and low temp. performance, high rate charge and discharge performance, and low maintenance cost.  It is expected to complement lithium-ion batteries in the field of large-scale electrochem. energy storage and low-speed elec. vehicles [1].  At present, the industrialization of sodium ion battery has started at home and abroad.  Sodium ion batteries have already had the market conditions and tech. conditions for large-scale industrialization.  This paper summarizes the structure of sodium ion batteries, materials, battery assembly and processing, and cost evaluation.  The bottlenecks in the development of sodium ion batteries and meaningful future research directions are summarized and personal insights are provided.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C7SE00120G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research and Application Progress on Key Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Sustain.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.05.089"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Eftekhari, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, D.-W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: New Opportunities Beyond Energy Storage by Lithium</span>. <i>J. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>395</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">336</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">348</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.05.089</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D395%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D336-348%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DA.%2BEftekhari%26author%3DD.-W.%2BKim%26title%3DSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BNew%2BOpportunities%2BBeyond%2BEnergy%2BStorage%2Bby%2BLithium%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2018.05.089&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c09706&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2018.05.089&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: New opportunities beyond energy storage by lithium</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Eftekhari, Ali; Kim, Dong-Won</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">395</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">336-348</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium has been recently attracted considerable attention as a promising charge carrier, but this sudden attention has made the strategy of research somewhat hazy, as most research reports are indeed the examn. of typical materials rather than following a solid roadmap for developing practical cells.  Although the history of sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) is as old as that of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), the potential of NIB had been neglected for decades until recently.  Most of the current electrode materials of NIBs have been previously examd. in LIBs.  Therefore, a better connection of these two sister energy storage systems can shed light on the possibilities for the pragmatic design of NIBs.  The first step is to realize the fundamental differences between the kinetics and thermodn. of Na as compared with those of Li.  In fact, tiny differences between the electrochem. behaviors of these systems can lead us to new practical ideas for designing suitable materials.  Furthermore, NIBs should be considered as new opportunities for energy storage rather than replacing LIBs.  Hence, the subtle strategy of research is to learn from LIBs but not replicate them when designing NIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/idm2.12048"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Discovery of Cathode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries from The View of Interdisciplinarity</span>. <i>Interdiscip.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent progress of emerging cathode materials for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Jun; Li, Xiao; Tang, Kaikai; Wang, Dandan; Long, Mengqi; Gao, Hong; Chen, Weihua; Liu, Chuntai; Liu, Hao; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Chemistry Frontiers</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Recently, due to the excessive consumption of fossil energy and the intermittent nature of clean energy resources, electricity storage has attracted great attention from both academia and industry.  Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) with high energy d. have been widely used for portable energy storage systems.  However, limited lithium resources cannot meet the demands for large-scale applications, esp. the grid-scale energy storage.  Owing to the abundant natural resources of sodium and the low price, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have become potential substitutes for LIBs.  Cathode materials are key components that det. the overall performance and entire cost of SIBs.  However, the unstable structure and limited electrochem. performance make them difficult to meet the requirement of practical applications.  This review summarizes the recent development of the cathode materials used for SIBs, and the modified strategies applied to improve their electrochem. performance.  Finally, crit. challenges and perspectives to promote the development of SIBs are also presented.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.3390/ma16216970"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alcántara, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pérez-Vicente, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lavela, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tirado, J.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review-research progress on layered transition metal oxide cathode materials for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Fanglin; Zhang, Qiaoping; Zhang, Peng; Tian, Wenqian; Dai, Kehua; Zhang, Liang; Mao, Jing; Shao, Guosheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">168</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The proportion of new energy power generation gets higher and higher, due to the depletion of fossil energy resources.  However, new energy power is generally unstable, so that it is necessary to use energy storage batteries to balance the power peak and valley.  Although lithium-ion batteries have been widely used in various fields, in particular for large-scale energy storage, the low abundance of lithium in the earth crust makes it untenable to meet the ever-intense future demand.  Sodium ion batteries, which have similar energy storage mechanism to lithium-ion batteries, have attracted significant attentions due to their abundant raw material resources, low cost, and fairly high energy densities.  Layered transition metal oxides are a class of the most promising cathode materials for sodium ion batteries, owing to their high theor. specific capacities, good cond., and fast diffusion kinetics.  In this paper, we conduct a comprehensive of the electrochem. performance, structural characteristics, performance shortcomings and modification technologies about the O3- and P2-type layered transition metal oxide cathode materials.  The application potentials of layered materials are summarized and analyzed, which provides a ref. for the industry to select the most promising and practical layered cathode material for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1134/S001670290601006X"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yaroshevsky, A.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Upper crustal abundances of trace elements: A revision and update</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Zhaochu; Gao, Shan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Geology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">253</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3-4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">205-221</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHGEAD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2541</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">New ests. are given of abundances of rarely analyzed elements (As, B, Be, Bi, Cd, Ge, In, Mo, Sb, Sn, Te, Tl, W) in the upper continental crust based on precise ICP-MS analyses of well-characterized upper crustal samples (shales, pelites, loess, graywackes, granitoids and their composites) from Australia, China, Europe, New Zealand and North American.  Obtaining a better understanding of the upper crustal abundance and assocd. uncertainties of these elements is important in placing better constraints on bulk crust compn. and, from that, whole Earth models of element cycling and crust generation.  Revised abundance ests. are also presented for some more commonly analyzed trace elements (Li, Cr, Ni, and Tm) that vary by &gt; 20% compared to previous ests.  The new ests. are mainly based on significant (r 2 &gt; 0.6) inter-element correlations obsd. in clastic sediments and sedimentary rocks, which yield upper continental crust elemental ratios that are used in conjunction with well-detd. abundances for certain key elements to place constraints on the concns. of the rarely analyzed elements.  Using the well-established upper crustal abundances of La (31 ppm), Th (10.5 ppm), Al2O3 (15.40%), K2O (2.80%) and Fe2O3 (5.92%), these ratios lead to revised upper crustal abundances of B = 47 ppm, Bi = 0.23 ppm, Cr = 73 ppm, Li = 41 ppm, Ni = 34 ppm, Sb = 0.075, Te = 0.027 ppm, Tl = 0.53 ppm and W = 1.4 ppm.  No significant correlations exist between Mo and Cd and other elements in the clastic sediments and sedimentary rocks, probably due to their enrichment in org. carbon.  Abundances of these elements are calcd. by assuming the upper continental crust consists of 65% granitoid rocks plus 35% clastic sedimentary rocks.  The validity of this approach is supported by the similarity of SiO2, Al2O3, La and Th abundances calcd. in this way with their upper crustal abundances given in Rudnick and Gao (2003).  The upper crustal abundances thus obtained are Mo = 0.6 ppm and Cd = 0.06 ppm.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.cossms.2012.04.002"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ellis, B. F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium and Sodium-Ion Energy Storage Batteries</span>. <i>Curr. Opin. Solid State Mater.                 10.1016/j.cossms.2012.04.002</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D16%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D168-177%26journal%3DCurr.%2BOpin.%2BSolid%2BState%2BMater.%2BSci.%26author%3DB.%2BL.%2BEllis%26author%3DL.%2BF.%2BNazar%26title%3DSodium%2Band%2BSodium-Ion%2BEnergy%2BStorage%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.cossms.2012.04.002&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c09706&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.cossms.2012.04.002&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium and sodium-ion energy storage batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ellis, Brian L.; Nazar, Linda F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Current Opinion in Solid State &amp; Materials Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Owing to almost unmatched volumetric energy d., Li-ion batteries have dominated the portable electronics industry and solid state electrochem. literature for the past 20 years.  Not only will that continue, but they are also now powering plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles and elec. vehicles.  In light of possible concerns over rising lithium costs in the future, Na and Na-ion batteries have re-emerged as candidates for medium and large-scale stationary energy storage, esp. as a result of heightened interest in renewable energy sources that provide intermittent power which needs to be load-levelled.  The sodium-ion battery field presents many solid state materials design challenges, and rising to that call in the past couple of years, several reports of new sodium-ion technologies and electrode materials have surfaced.  These range from high-temp. air electrodes to new layered oxides, polyanion-based materials, carbons and other insertion materials for sodium-ion batteries, many of which hold promise for future sodium-based energy storage applications.  In this article, the challenges of current high-temp. sodium technologies including Na-S and Na-NiCl2 and new molten sodium technol., Na-O2 are summarized.  Recent advancements in pos. and neg. electrode materials suitable for Na-ion and hybrid Na/Li-ion cells are reviewed, along with the prospects for future developments.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-Temperature Stationary Sodium-Ion Batteries for Large-Scale Electric Energy Storage</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1039/C8EE03727B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ling, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exploring Competitive Features of Stationary Sodium Ion Batteries for Electrochemical Energy Storage</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring competitive features of stationary sodium ion batteries for electrochemical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Tiefeng; Zhang, Yaping; Jiang, Zhanguo; Zeng, Xianqing; Ji, Jiapeng; Li, Zeheng; Gao, Xuehui; Sun, Minghao; Lin, Zhan; Ling, Min; Zheng, Junchao; Liang, Chengdu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the excellent abundance and availability of sodium reserves, sodium ion batteries (NIBs) show great promise for meeting the material supply and cost demands of large-scale energy storage systems (ESSs) used for the application of renewable energy sources and smart grids.  However, the cost advantages of stationary NIBs alone are not enough to ensure their com. success.  In this review, we summarize the emerging attractive characteristics of stationary NIBs, such as high-rate capability, all-climate operation, and full-battery recyclability.  Together with inherent cost advantages, these merits have resulted in an excellent compatibility between stationary NIBs and large-scale ESSs that dictates their validity in practical applications.  Representative electrode materials are highlighted to illustrate advances in corresponding features.  The insights presented in this review can inspire further research interest into NIB design and serve as a guide for the application of NIBs in large-scale stationary energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202001274"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hirsh, H.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries Paving the Way for Grid Energy Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hirsh, Hayley S.; Li, Yixuan; Tan, Darren H. S.; Zhang, Minghao; Zhao, Enyue; Meng, Y. Shirley</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">32</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2001274</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The recent proliferation of renewable energy generation offers mankind hope, with regard to combating global climate change.  However, reaping the full benefits of these renewable energy sources requires the ability to store and distribute any renewable energy generated in a cost-effective, safe, and sustainable manner.  As such, sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have been touted as an attractive storage technol. due to their elemental abundance, promising electrochem. performance and environmentally benign nature.  Moreover, new developments in sodium battery materials have enabled the adoption of high-voltage and high-capacity cathodes free of rare earth elements such as Li, Co, Ni, offering pathways for low-cost NIBs that match their lithium counterparts in energy d. while serving the needs for large-scale grid energy storage.  In this essay, a range of battery chemistries are discussed alongside their resp. battery properties while keeping metrics for grid storage in mind.  Matters regarding materials and full cell cost, supply chain and environmental sustainability are discussed, with emphasis on the need to eliminate several elements (Li, Ni, Co) from NIBs.  Future directions for research are also discussed, along with potential strategies to overcome obstacles in battery safety and sustainable recyclability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700463"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muñoz-Márquez, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  This paper gives an overview of the research carried out on lithium and sodium layered materials as pos. electrodes of lithium (sodium)-ion batteries.  It focuses on the solid-state chem. contribution to discover new materials and to optimize the properties vs. the requirements imposed by the applications.  Among, all material structures, which are considered, the layered ones (lithium based), are the best candidates for high energy d. batteries for mobile applications.  Recently, the homologous Na materials, which have lower energy, are considered for stationary applications due to their low price.  Starting for LiMO2 materials or NaxMO2 (0.5 &lt; x &lt; 1), many substituted phases, obtained by high-temp. solid-state chem., have allowed stabilizing the layered structure in large compn. domains to increase the specific capacity, which is directly related to the no. of exchanged electrons during the cycling process.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b03937"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Comprehensive Review of P2-Type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, a Potential Cathode for Practical Application of Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Comprehensive Review of P2-Type Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2, a Potential Cathode for Practical Application of Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Jiaolong; Wang, Wenhui; Wang, Wei; Wang, Shuwei; Li, Baohua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  P2-type Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 is a promising cathode material for practical applications in Na-ion batteries, due to its high energy d., high volumetric capacity, excellent Na ion cond., ease of synthesis, and good stability in air.  Yet, it is subject to structural rearrangements on charging to high voltage/low Na content and Na+/vacancy ordering transitions, which lead to poor reversibility and dramatic capacity decay upon cycling.  In this review, the latest advances related to Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 are presented with a main focus on strategies to stabilize the structural framework and improve the electrochem. properties.  Practical issues and challenges are also proposed on the basis of current research status and progress.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA11927A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-Type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as a Cathode Material with High-Rate and Long-Life for Sodium Ion Storage</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-type Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 as a cathode material with high-rate and long-life for sodium ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Qiannan; Hu, Zhe; Chen, Mingzhe; Zou, Chao; Jin, Huile; Wang, Shun; Gu, Qinfen; Chou, Shulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered P2-type Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 was successfully synthesized through a facile sol-gel method and subsequent heat treatment.  Resulting from different phase transformation and sodium ion diffusion rates, its electrochem. performance is highly related to the cut-off voltage and the electrolyte used.  When the cut-off voltage is set up to 4.5 V or lowered to 1.5 V, capacity fade happens due to the occurrence of P2-O2 transformation and electrolyte decompn. or the redox reaction of the Mn4+ / Mn3+ ionic pair and P2-P2' transformation.  The electrode maintained 89.0 mA h g-1 with good cycling stability and excellent structural preservation between 4.0 and 2.0 V.  The capacity retention is 71.2% even after 1200 cycles at 10C.  It can be expected that P2-type Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 is promising as a cathode material for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1149/2.0201514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Clément, R.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">1D to 2D Na+ ion diffusion inherently linked to structural transitions in Na0.7CoO2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Medarde, M.; Mena, M.; Gavilano, J. R.; Mansson, M.; Juranyi, F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report the observation of a stepwise "melting" of the low-temp. Na-vacancy order in the layered transition-metal oxide Na0.7CoO2.  High-resoln. neutron powder diffraction anal. indicates the existence of two first-order structural transitions, one at T1 ≈ 290 K followed by a second at T2 ≈ 400 K.  Detailed anal. strongly suggests that both transitions are linked to changes in the Na mobility.  The data are consistent with a two-step disappearance of Na-vacancy order through the successive opening of first quasi-1D (T1 &gt; T &gt; T2) and then 2D (T &gt; T2) Na diffusion paths.  The results shed new light on previous, seemingly incompatible, exptl. interpretations regarding the relationship between Na-vacancy order and Na dynamics in this material.  They also represent an important step towards the tuning of phys. properties and the design of tailored functional materials through an improved control and understanding of ionic diffusion.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2013.09.098"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, X.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.-F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Properties of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>) O<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries when Cycled in Different Voltage Ranges</span>. <i>Electrochim.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Oxygen-Storage Materials to Stabilize the Oxygen Redox Activity of Three-Layered Sodium Transition Metal Oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kalapsazova, Mariya L.; Kostov, Krassimir L.; Kukeva, Rositsa R.; Zhecheva, Ekaterina N.; Stoyanova, Radostina K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">32</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7804-7811</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCLCD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1948-7185</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To double the energy d. of lithium- and sodium-ion batteries there is a need to activate simultaneously cationic and anionic redox reactions at the intercalation-type electrodes.  In contrast to the cationic redox activity, the oxygen redox activity enforces an enhancement in the surface reactivity of the oxides leading to their poor reversibility and cycling stability.  Herein, we propose a new concept to stabilize oxygen redox activity by using oxygen-storage materials as an efficient buffer supplying and receiving oxygen during alkali ion intercalation.  As a proof-of-concept, the study is focused on CeO2 as a modifier of sodium nickel-manganese oxide with a three-layer sequence, P3-Na2/3Ni1/2Mn1/2O2.  The CeO2-modified P3-Na2/3Ni1/2Mn1/2O2 displays a drastic increase in the reversible capacity following the order Na+ intercalation &lt; Li+ intercalation &lt; Li+,Na+ cointercalation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.06.026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Enaya, H. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Layered P2- Na<sub>2/3</sub>(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> as High-Voltage Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries: The Capacity Decay Mechanism and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> Surface Modification</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>27</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">27</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">34</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.06.026</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D27%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D27-34%26journal%3DNano%2BEnergy%26author%3DY.%2BLiu%26author%3DX.%2BFang%26author%3DA.%2BZhang%26author%3DC.%2BShen%26author%3DQ.%2BLiu%26author%3DH.%2BA.%2BEnaya%26author%3DC.%2BZhou%26title%3DLayered%2BP2-%2BNa2%252F3%2528Ni1%252F3Mn2%252F3%2529O2%2Bas%2BHigh-Voltage%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BThe%2BCapacity%2BDecay%2BMechanism%2Band%2BAl2O3%2BSurface%2BModification%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nanoen.2016.06.026&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c09706&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nanoen.2016.06.026&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                        cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Controlled design of metal oxide-based (Mn2+/Nb5+) anodes for superior sodium-ion hybrid supercapacitors: Synergistic mechanisms of hybrid ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qin, Jian; Sari, Hirbod Maleki Kheimeh; Wang, Xiujuan; Yang, Huijuan; Zhang, Jiujun; Li, Xifei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">71</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">104594</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, metal oxides (Mn2+/Nb5+) embedded into interconnected hollow carbon nanoboxes (MnO@HCNb and NaNbO3@HCNb) are fabricated by a simple template-assisted CVD method.  Such a unique structure provides shortened solid-state transportation pathway and sufficient adsorption sites for Na+, with differing energy storage mechanisms from traditional Faradic reaction.  Benefiting from it, the MnO@HCNb electrode exhibits an outstanding rate performance and an ultra-long lifespan of more than 10,000 cycles with 88.6% of capacity retention, which is rarely reported for oxide-based anodes.  Moreover, energy storage mechanism of the composite and the important role of Mn/Na2O interface generated inside the core (MnO) - shell (C) structure during conversion reaction have been revealed.  Hybrid ion storage mechanisms (adsorption, Faradic reaction and intercalation) are proposed with desirable compatibility, exhibiting uniform capacitive electrochem. behavior.  Coupling with activated carbon cathode, a full cell of Na-HSC demonstrates a high energy d. of 116 Wh kg-1 at 99 W kg-1 and 56.4 Wh kg-1 at 1.8 kW kg-1.  Indeed, this work is inspiring to other researchers to further improve the electrochem. performance of anode materials by adjusting their energy storage mechanisms through structural reconstruction.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1016/j.jcis.2023.12.008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Simultaneously Promoting the Surface/Bulk Structural Stability of Fe/Mn-Based Layered Cathode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered P2-Nax[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 (0 &lt; x &lt; 2/3) was studied as a cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  A combination of 1st principles computation, electrochem. and synchrotron characterizations is conducted to elucidate the working mechanism for the improved electrochem. properties.  The reversible phase transformation from P2 to O2 is obsd.  New configurations of Na-ions and vacancy are found at x = 1/3 and 1/2, which correspond to the intermediate phases upon the electrochem. cycling process.  The mobility of Na-ions is studied using the galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique (GITT) and the Na diffusion barriers are calcd. by the Nudged Elastic Band (NEB) method.  Both techniques prove that the mobility of Na-ions is faster than Li-ions in the O3 structure within the 1/3 &lt; x &lt; 2/3 concn. region.  Excellent cycling properties and high rate capability can be obtained by limiting the O framework shift during P2-O2 phase transformation, suggesting that this material can be a strong candidate as a sustainable low-cost Na-ion battery cathode.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref44" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201400083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hasa, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Scrosati, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To realize a reversible solid-state MnIII/IV redox couple in layered oxides, co-operative Jahn-Teller distortion (CJTD) of six-coordinate MnIII (t2g3-eg1) is a key factor in terms of structural and phys. properties.  We develop a single-phase synthesis route for two polymorphs, namely distorted and undistorted P2-type Na2/3MnO2 having different Mn stoichiometry, and investigate how the structural and stoichiometric difference influences electrochem. reaction.  The distorted Na2/3MnO2 delivers 216 mAh g-1 as a 3 V class pos. electrode, reaching 590 Wh (kg oxide)-1 with excellent cycle stability in a non-aq. Na cell and demonstrates better electrochem. behavior compared to undistorted Na2/3MnO2. As sodium is reinserted in [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 to make Na1/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 the P2 structure forms again.  The evolution of the lattice parameters of P2-Nax[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 (1/3 &lt; x &lt; 2/3) with x and the lattice consts. of [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 are also reported.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref47" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b18493"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jia, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Identifying Anionic Redox Activity within the Related O3- and P2-Type Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Battery</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Identifying Anionic Redox Activity within the Related O3- and P2-Type Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jia, Min; Qiao, Yu; Li, Xiang; Qiu, Feilong; Cao, Xin; He, Ping; Zhou, Haoshen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">851-857</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As promising cathodes for Na-ion batteries (NIBs), layered transition-metal (TM) oxides have attracted intense research activities because of high specific capacities, esp. benefiting from the boosted capacity triggered by oxygen-related anionic redox reactions (ARRs).  However, regarding ARRs activity, the difference between typical O3- and P2-type structures has not been clarified with in-depth exploration.  Herein, composed with similar compn., ARRs-induced oxygen behaviors within O3-Na0.6Li0.2Fe0.4Ru0.4O2 and P2-Na0.6Li0.35Fe0.1Ru0.55O2 are systematically investigated by varying ex/in situ spectroscopic characterizations.  Conducted with a lower charging cutoff voltage (4.0 V), P2-type cathode will more easily trigger the reversible oxygen behaviors and deliver a larger capacity, better rate performance, and stable cyclability, in contrast to the O3-type cathode.  Moreover, within O3-type structure, increasing charging potential (beyond 4.3 V) would induce addnl. anionic oxidn. capacity, but inevitably lead to the irreversible evolution of gaseous O2 and superoxo.  With the unique feature, this work provides a promising strategy design for fabricating cathodes with optimal microstructural arrangement, which could further push forward the changes in macro-/nanostructures and even ideal performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201802379"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sathiya, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mendoza-Sánchez, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iadecola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vergnet, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dedryvère, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saubanère, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abakumov, A.                         cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Templated Nanocrystal-Based Porous TiO2 Films for Next-Generation Electrochemical Capacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brezesinski, Torsten; Wang, John; Polleux, Julien; Dunn, Bruce; Tolbert, Sarah H.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">131</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1802-1809</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The advantages in using nanoscale materials for electrochem. energy storage are generally attributed to short diffusion path lengths for both electronic and lithium ion transport.  Here, another contribution, the charge storage from faradaic processes occurring at the surface, referred to as pseudocapacitive effect are considered.  This paper describes the synthesis and pseudocapacitive characteristics of block copolymer templated anatase TiO2 thin films synthesized using either sol-gel reagents or preformed nanocrystals as building blocks.  Both materials are highly cryst. and have large surface areas; however, the structure of the porosity is not identical.  The different titania systems were characterized by a combination of small- and wide-angle x-ray diffraction/scattering, combined with SEM imaging and physisorption measurements.  Following the authors' previously reported approach, the authors are able to use the voltammetric sweep rate dependence to det. quant. the capacitive contribution to the current response.  Considerable enhancement of the electrochem. properties results when the films are both made from nanocrystals and mesoporous.  Such materials show high levels of capacitive charge storage and high insertion capacities.  By contrast, when mesoscale porosity is created in a material with dense walls (rather than porous walls derived from the aggregation of nanocrystals), insertion capacities comparable to templated nanocrystal films can be achieved, but the capacitance is much lower.  The results presented here illustrate the importance of pseudocapacitive behavior that develops in high surface area mesoporous oxide films.  Such systems provide a new class of pseudocapacitive materials, which offer increased charge storage without compromising charge storage kinetics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref51" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2018.04.150"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Valvo, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Doubaji, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saadoune, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Edström, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Pseudocapacitive Charge Storage Properties of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Co<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/9</sub>Ni<sub>1/9</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim.                 10.1016/j.electacta.2018.04.150</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D276%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D142-152%26journal%3DElectrochim.%2BActa%26author%3DM.%2BValvo%26author%3DS.%2BDoubaji%26author%3DI.%2BSaadoune%26author%3DK.%2BEdstr%25C3%25B6m%26title%3DPseudocapacitive%2BCharge%2BStorage%2BProperties%2Bof%2BNa2%252F3Co2%252F3Mn2%252F9Ni1%252F9O2%2Bin%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.electacta.2018.04.150&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c09706&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.electacta.2018.04.150&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

E. B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Cathode Ray Polarograph. Part II.─The Current-Voltage Curves</span>. <i>Trans.
